STATE SUPERINTENDENTS OF PUBLIC INSTRUCTION 
The office of State Superintendent of Public Instruction was created in 
1835, and by an Act of the Legislature of 1843, which went into effect in 1844, 
the office was abolished. The following Superintendents were elected by the 
Legislature: 
Rosert H. McEwen, 1835-1840. 
Roserr P. Currin, 1840-1841. 
Scorr Terry, 1841-1844. 
From 1844 to 1867 the State Treasurer was ex-officio Superintendent of 
Public Instruction. In 1867 the office was again created. The following 
Superintendents were elected by the people, and served for the respective 
periods shown below: 
Joun Eaton, Jr., 1867-1869. 
A. J. Treron, 1869-1871. 
In 1870 the office was again abolished, and the duties devolved upon the 
State Treasurer, who was made ex-officio Superintendent of Public Instruction. 
In 1873 the office was recreated, and the following officers, appointed by 
the Governor and confirmed by the Senate, have served the terms ‘shown 
below: 
*J. B. KitLeprew. 
Joun M. Fiemine, 1873-1875. 
Leon Trouspate, 1875-1881. 
**W._S. Doak, 1881-1882. - 
1G. 8. W. Crawrorp, 1882-1883. 
Tuos. H. Paine, 1883-1887. 
Frank M. Smirtn, 1887-1891. 
W. R. Garrett, 1891-1893. 
Franx M. Smiru, 1893-1895. 
S. G. Grtpreata, 1895-1897. 
Price Tuomas, 1897-1899, - 
Morean C. Fitzpatrick, 1899-1903. 
Seymour A. Mynners, 1903-1907. 
R. L. Jones, 1907-1911. 
J. W. Brisrer, 1911-1913. 
S. H. Toompson, 1913-1915. 
S. W. Suerritt, 1915-1919, 
Apert Wittiams, 1919-1921. 
J. B. Brown, 1921-1923. 
Under the reorganization bill of 1923 the State Department of Public 
Instruction became the State Department of Education. The title of the 
chief officer was changed to Commissioner of Education. 
P. L. Harnep, 1923- 
*Assistant State Superintendent, 1870-1873. 
**Died in office. 
+Filled unexpired term.

INTRODUCTION 
REORGANIZATION OF THE DEPARTMENT 
The Reorganization Act of 1923, which became effective Feburary 1, 1923, 
creates the Department of Education which includes the following divisions: 
(1) elementary schools, (2) high schools, (3) certification of teachers, (4) voca- 
tional education, (5) library and archives, (6) geology, (7) registration of pro- 
fessions and trades. The duties of the land office, the board of preliminary 
medical examiners and the historical commission were also transferred to the 
Department, but not as separate divisions. 
The Act not only makes it possible for a better organization of the agencies 
of the various divisions, but it has resulted in a material reduction of salary, 
and office and traveling expense, as is shown by the following comparison 
for the years February 1, 1922, to January 31, 1923, and February 1, 1923, to 
January 31, 1924: 
, 1922-1923 1923-1924 
1. Commissioner of Education Salary and Traveling Expenses_$ 6,106.02 $ 5,902.07

However, the foregoing does not show the full reduction for the reason that 
© certain items under the 1921 appropriation bill had to be carried on until the 
end of the fiscal year, June 30, 1923. The increase in the State Library divi- 
sion is due to transferring the history work which was formerly done under a 
separate division. 
STATISTICAL TABLES 
While it has been impossible to obtain complete reports from every county, 
all the statistics are compiled from reports made by county superintendents 
» under oath as the law directs. Nothing has been estimated by the Depart- 
> ment. 
    The obvious deficiencies are due to the following causes: (1) The records 
in one county were destroyed by fire and the superintendent was unable to 
reproduce them accurately. (2) One county failed to take the scholastic popu- 
lation. (3) The enumerators in several counties made very inaccurate re- 
’ ports, especially as to the number unable to read and write. (4) The financial 
tables probably show somewhat more school funds than were collected by 
some county trustees. In such instances the trustee does not keep and report 
the cash actually collected and distributed, but the school fund, collected and 
uncollected, charged against him before the quarterly court makes its annual

19— 
SPECIAL LEGISLATION 
During the past fifteen years, the passage of local school laws has done 
much to retard progress. If a superintendent is popular with the quarterly 
court but unpopular with the community or vice versa, because of some per- 
fectly sane act of his, the community or quarterly court may proceed to have 
a law passed that will change the method of electing in order to defeat him. 
The same thing sometimes happens to a board of education, and the general 
law governing the board’s membership or election is changed to suit a par- 
ticular county. Again any community that desires it, because of certain 
advantages to itself, may, by special legislation, be made into a special taxing 
district and assume full control of its school and all funds belonging thereto. 
In such cases a special act may weaken half a dozen other schools around 
this unit or perhaps all the other schools of the county. Such legislation is 
wrong in principle as well as in the method of correcting the “evil”, real or 
imaginary. There is always a regular method of correcting unsatisfactory 
conditions without resorting to local legislation. By working together as a 
county unit alltheschools can be brought uptothe desired standard. Besides 
it is of equal importance to the State and the county that equal educational 
opportunity be given all children out of public funds. The people of any com- 
munity, under general laws, may supplement the funds of their school to 
extend the term or to improve its teaching force without interfering with the 
state or county school system. In nearly every case of local legislation some 
community, or the county as a whole, is weakened more than the special 
district is helped. 
ELEMENTARY SCHOOL TERM 
Before the efficiency of all the rural schools can be improved very much, it 
will be necessary for the State to extend the term uniformly to a minimum of 
eight months. The people demand skilled teachers and they have a right to 
make such demands, but before they can secure such service in all the rural 
schools the term must be made longer. The necessity for a longer term is 
obvious for two reasons. (1) It will take the average child at least eight 
months to complete a grade. If the term is shorter the work cannot be com- 
pleted in a year. The short device is not economy, for every child’s time is 
valuable; and the aggregate waste in time and money, because of short terms, 
result in a great loss to the State. (2) Teachers who have spirit, ability and 
skill will not remain in a position that offers only six months’ work a year. A 
* majority of the skilled teachers have completed a first-class high school course 
and a course in the normal school or college. To do this costs money and seven 
or eight years’ work, which is the teacher’s investment. He cannot afford to 
make such investment if his future profits depend on a short-term school. 
The elementary schools must’be standardized, but this cannot be done if 
the term in one county is five months and in another nine, nor can it be done 
with teachers of low grade of efficiency in one county and high grade in another. 
Every child should be given a well directed elementary education. High 
school and college education is an opportunity, an elementary education is a 
necessity. While the high schools, which reach a small per cent of the children, 
should continue their high standard, yet the elementary schools, which reach 
all the children, must be raised to’even a higher standard of efficiency. The 
first step necessary to do this is to make the minimum term eight months.in all 
the counties.
-P-A-G-E-20
—20— 
Savaries OF ELEMENTARY TEACHERS 
A rural elementary school teacher is entitled to a salary commensurate 
with the service he renders the State. The “basis” for fixing teachers’ salaries 
is not conducive to better.work. If the untrained and inefficient person is 
paid as much salary as is offered the skilled teacher, skill is driven out of the 
State by such competition. If the high school offers a higher salary than the 
rural school offers, the most efficient rural teachers leave the country school 
and go to the high school. Thus it is becoming more and more impossible to 
secure capable teachers for country schools. The salary of the skilled teacher, 
who goes to the remotest rural community, should be at least as much as that 
of the high school teacher of equal training and skill, for the service of the 
‘teacher who goes to the remote community and gives life to its school is worth 
more to the State than that of the teacher who works in the high school. 
With the aid of the University of Tennessee and Peabody College for 
Teachers, the Department has worked out a standard which is now being 
tested by a number of counties. When these tests are returned furtherstudy 
will be made for positive proof of efficiency. Then it will be given all counties, 
and a uniform, reliable, just basis will be established throughout the State 
—for equal training and service, equal minimum salaries will be paid by all 
counties. A county or a city should be permitted to supplement the minimum 
as much as it desires. 
Scnoot Houses anp EquipMENT 
This subject has failed to impress itself on the minds of the people in rural 
sections.. Some counties have built magnificient court houses, comfortable 
jails and good roads, while they have left the poor little uncomfortable, in- 
sanitary school house as a marker of the lack of interest in the school home of 
their children. In a few counties the court house alone cost the taxpayers 
more than the estimated value of all school houses, grounds and equipment. 
In one county one mile of ordinary country road is said to have cost the 
county more than the estimated value of all school property of that county. 
While the building and equipping of school houses is the function of the 
county, yet many counties have no means of making such improvements ex- 
cept out of a land tax. They have no public utilities to tax, nothing but farm 
lands. Some of the counties, perhaps unfortunately, are already carrying large 
bond issues to be paid by the next generation. Good roads are necessary— 
so are good school houses. If road building is a state obligation, so is the 
building of school houses. If one is a greater obligation than the other, is it 
not the school house? If the State should issue bonds for roads, it should at 
the same time issue bonds for school buildings—in such amounts and at 
such intervals as will guarantee the most economical and best use of such 
building funds, 
Course or Srupy 
From time to time the legislature has added subjects to the elementary 
course of study until it is a physical impossibility for one teacher to teach 
them all, or for any child to learn them all within the time allotted. The 
course of study should be thoroughly revised and made possible for both the 
teacher and the pupil. 
It is a waste of time and money to require teachers in one-teacher schools 
 
-P-A-G-E-21
—21— 
to try to teach all the subjects now required by law to be taught, and the law 
must be changed so as to limit the work of the teacher and the pupil to what is 
possible. However, notwithstanding this unfortunate condition, the patrons 
of many schools are clamoring for their children to.study high school branches 
in the elementary schools, and some boards of education are’ permitting it, 
It is needless to repeat that, under such conditions, neither the work of 
the teacher nor the pupil can be satisfactory. 
Text Booxs 
Under the present textbook law it is difficult to make the best 'selection of 
books in every case and impossible to save the purchaser more than 5% on list 
price. By changing from an indirect to a direct method of distribution, one 
handling of books, one transportation charge, and one merchant’s profit can 
be saved. In other words, if the law is so changed that books can be shipped 
direct to local dealers instead of through depositories to local dealers, at least 
13% of the list price can be saved to the purchaser, or if books were handled 
by cities or counties as is done in some states, even more can be saved. 
A revision ofthe present law, changing the method of distribution of text- 
books, would save the State in round numbers nearly half a million dollars 
during a single contract period. 
CuassiFIcaTion oF County Hicu Scuoois 
Before a county board can establish a first class county high school under. 
the present law, it must provide a minimum of three teachers who meet the 
requirements made by the State Board of Education. This makes the cost of 
operating a first class high school with few pupils entirely too high. 
Under another section of the school law, boards of education are permitted 
to use elementary school funds for teaching two years of high school work. 
This law was intended to provide instruction formerly provided under the 
secondary school law. In practically every place where high school work is 
done in a rural elementary school it is done at the expense of the children in 
the grades. Therefore, this law should be so changed that no elementary 
school funds can be used to employ teachers of high school subjects. 
County Scuoo. Tax Levy 
Under the present tax system, county superintendents should work out. 
their financial needs at least two years ahead. It is important that they take 
into consideration the reduction of assessments and see that their respective 
Quarterly Courts increase the school tax rate sufficiently to take care of the 
needs of all their schools for the term and at the salary rate they desire. Be- 
cause of a considerable reduction in the assessment of the State there will be a 
deficiency in the school funds produced by the county unless superintendents 
bring this matter forcibly to the attention at the Quarterly Courts at the. 
July term, : 
CERTIFICATION OF TEACHERS 
The laws regulating the issuance of teachers and county superintendents 
certificates have been amended so many times and in so many different ways 
that.they operate in favor of the untrained teacher and superintendent. For 
example, a graduate of a normal school.can be given a professional license to
-P-A-G-E-22
22 
teach only in schools below high schools.of the first class, while an eighteen 
year old graduate of a first-class high school who has had eight months’. ex- 
perience as a teacher may take the high school examination in one or more 
groups, and, if an average of 85 per cent is made, may receive a license to teach 
in any public high school. This limitation should be taken off graduates of 
the normal schools. After they have had three full years of training they 
should be permitted to teach in high schools of the first class without examina- 
tion. Those who have had only a course in a county high school ought not to 
be licensed even on examination to teach in high schools. 
With respect to licensing county scliool superintendents the law should be 
changed so that professional certificates might be issued only to those appli- 
cants who have completed a full course in school law and school administra- 
tion. ‘The county superintendent is the most important administrative officer 
in the public school system and should have such special training as will prepare 
him to invest the school funds wisely as well as to direct the activities of the 
supervisors and the teachers. Further emphasis should be laid on-school law 
and administration in licensing applicants for superintendents’ certificatés on 
examination. : 
Divivep Scnoot TERM 
In certain counties of the State, especially cotton counties, the elementary 
school term is divided into two parts. The schools are opened early in the 
summer, run a few months, suspend a few months, then reopen and remain in 
session until the funds are used. Children can do much more within six. or 
seven months if the term is continuous. The cause of this suspension seems 
to be the desire of the farmers to close their schools during the period of 
harvesting certain crops. Since such suspension of the schools breaks up the 
work of all the children soon after they have started the year, it seems that 
some provision should be made by the farmers to take care of farm work in 
such way that the education of their children would not be thus interrupted. 
To consider the interest of the children as a whole, it is my opinion that every 
school should run continuously until the funds for a given year are used. The 
farmers in the tobacco section of the State have as good reason to demand the 
suspension of their schools during a period of two months, but they have never 
formed the habit of asking a suspension and they make their plans to keep a 
large majority of their children in school all the time. County superintendents 
and county boards of education in. all the counties should devise plans for 
running their schools the full term without intermission. 
Unirorm Promotion or ELementary Pupits 
The law provides that all the schools of the county should run as nearly as 
possible the same number of days. When this law was enacted the reason for. 
any discretionary power within the Board was that if the attendance of any 
school fell below the minimum fixed by the law such school should be closed. 
Since that time the compulsory attendance law has been enacted and now 
superintendents and county boards of education can compel sufficient at- 
tendance to prevent the closing of a school. Therefore, there is no obvious 
reason why all the schools of a county may not run the same number of days. 
They should start on the same day and end on the same day in order that 
promotions in all the grades may be made on a uniform examination at the
-P-A-G-E-23
—23— 
close of the term. The county superintendent should prepare the questions 
for each grade and should make the regulations governing the examination in 
all the schools of his county. 
Scnoot ArcniITEcT 
There are a number of school buildings erected in the State every year. 
Usually buildings costing $10,000.00 or more are planned by some architect. 
The fees of an architect are three per cent of the cost of a building for plans 
and specifications and two per cent for supervision of construction, making a 
total of five per cent. This amounts to a very large sum every year. The 
State had to pay last year about $32,000.00 architect fees on the buildings at 
the normal schools. It would be economy to employ a State architect, on a 
salary, to draw plans and make specifications for the school buildings in all 
the counties. Such arrangement would save a vast sum of both elementary 
and high school funds every year in addition to having available expert 
knowledge of modern school building which would guarantee the greatest con- 
venience as well as the protection of the health of school children. 
SPEecIAL Funps 
By the aid of $65,148.68 furnished by philanthropic agencies the State has 
been enabled to supplement its work in the following special fields: 
1. The General Education Board, New York, pays the salary and traveling expenses of two 
elementary school agents, one high school agent and one stenographer, who work under 
the direction of the State Department of Education. The annual salary and expenses 
of these agents amounts to $16,800.00. In addition to this, $12,400.00 was provided 
for the school year 1922-23 to aid in the maintenance of fifteen county training schools 
for negroes, which schools specialize in industrial education. 
2. Within the year, the State received from the Julius Rosenwald Fund $21,800.00 for the 
construction of negro school houses. Twenty-eight new buildings were completed and 
additions made to three old buildings. . 
‘The entire cost of these buildings and additions is $115,689.00, of which amount $27,761.00 
was from the negroes, $565.00 from miscellaneous sources, $66,642.00 from public 
funds, $21,800.00 from the Julius Rosenwald Fund. - : 
3. The Jeanes Fund is used by local school officials in the employment of specially trained 
teachers for negroes in order to promote industrial and health education. The State 
received $7,000.00 from this source. 
4. The Slater Fund amounting to $6,300.00 is used in county training schools for negroes 
under the general management of the local school board. Seven towns and fifteen 
counties are now receiving benefit from this fund. 
The State gratefully acknowledges this aid. 
Scuoo. Funps 
It is not a difficult task to locate the weaknesses of elementary schools.and. 
to find what must be done to improve their efficiency, but it will require both 
time and money to do it. If time is used under impossible conditions it 
counts but little. Unless there is enough money put into a business to operate 
it successfully what is put into it counts for little if any profit. . 
< There is no good reason for waiting indefinitely to standardize the rural. 
schools. Nearly all the people are depending on them to educate their chil+ 
dren. - Shall they wait for the completion of every other public service?’ Un-
-P-A-G-E-24
—24— 
less these schools are made to meet the needs, this State can never be the 
greatest state. But it will require more money to do the work. 
At present the elementary school revenues are derived from the following 
sources: 
STATE 
1. Interest on the permanent school fund amounting to $150,750.00 annually. This fund is 
constitutional and cannot be changed by statutes. 
2. Eight cents on the hundred dollars of taxable property amounting to $1,373,154.47 
3. One-third of the gross revenue of the state including excise tax, but excepting express, sleep—| 
ing car, news, terminal, telephone, telegraph, and insurance companies, amounts 
to $1,857,294.05. 
The foregoing amounts are distributed as follows: 
Normal Schools. 
High Schools... 
Supplement County 
Vocational Education 
State Department, salaries and operating expenses_ 
Rural School Libraries 
State Board of Education 
          
    The remainder goes to elementary schools, $2,000.00 to every county as an equalizing 
fund and $6,000.00 to every county levying a tax of 30 cents in addition to the State 
levy, unless a lower rate produces $5.00 for each person of school age. After deducting 
the above the balance is distributed among the counties on the basis of average daily 
attendance. 
4, Ten cents on all property, under Chapter 25, Acts of 1873. 
5. One-half the merchants’ privilege tax. 
6. Two dollars on each marriage license. 
7. One dollar on every poll tax. 
Numbers 4, 5, 6, and 7, are not paid into the State Treasury but are held and used in the 
county where collected. 
COUNTY 
1, Property tax varying from 2414 cents to 75 cents on the $100.00. 
2. Poll tax of $1.00. 
3. Marriage license. 
4. One-half merchants’ privilege tax. 
All the county levies and collects for school purposes is held and used in the county where 
collected. 
To operate all the county and city schools eight months at an average 
annual salary of $800.00 would require $11,000.000.00 salary, and $2,500.000.00 
for operating expenses. While it may seem a large sum, yet it is much less 
‘than the federal tax the State is paying. During the past fiscal year the 
people of the State paid $17,995,959.32 direct federal tax. What is spent for 
schools wisely is not a tax but an investment. 
It is unfair to require property almost wholly to bear the support of pub~ 
lic schools. Others than property owners have children to educate and should 
pay their just proportion. Those who spend money for luxuries and those who | 
are fortunate enough to have substantial incomes should also contribute to 
the future citizenship of the State. 
The State owes an equal educational opportunity to all her children. The 
financing of public education is a state rather than a local duty, and the total 
property and the total adult population should contribute to its support.
-P-A-G-E-25
—25— 
In conclusion the following recommendation are most respectfully sub- 
mitted: 
A complete revision of the public school laws, including in such revision 
1. A more economic and business like county administration. 
2. An eight-months minimum school term for all counties. 
3. One superintendent for county and town, large cities excepted. 
4. Salary of superintendents and teachers, determined by fair standards, commensurate with 
their service. : 
5. Repeal of all local legislation, large cities excepted. 
6. Complete revision of the elementary course of study, reducing the number of subjects to 
what is physically possible for teacher and pupil. 
7. Better care of school property with adequate provisions for replacement and improvement. 
8. Proper distribution of school tax levies among all classes of people. 
9. Change in method of selecting and distributing textbooks to insure better selection and a - 
greater saving to the people. 
10. Revision of all laws bearing on the licensing of county superintendents and teachers. 
11. Continuous school term for the year. 
12. Uniform promotion of chidren in all grades based on examination at close of term. 
13. A state school architect for economy and efficiency. 
14, Equal educational opportunities for all children. 
Most respectfully, 
P. L. Harnep, Commissioner.
-P-A-G-E-26

Division of Vocational Education 
Agriculture, Home Economics, Trades, and Industries 
 
-P-A-G-E-168
SUMMARY OF REPORT 
DIVISION OF VOCATIONAL EDUCATION 
IN 
VOCATIONAL AGRICULTURE 
1923 
The agricultural education situation within the State of Tennessee is the 
most promising of any year since the organization. With seventeen counties 
in West Tennessee, seventeen counties in Middle Tennessee, and fifteen 
counties in East Tennessee, the work is becoming too popular for our financial 
condition. At present there are ten new counties applying for the work and 
many counties that already have it are anxious to secure the services of addi- 
tional men. 
The most important development during the year has been the évening 
class work. Sixty-six courses have been conducted with farmers in thirty-nine 
schools and one thousand two hundred and sixty-four were in attendance. In 
every instance the farmers are carrying projects and showing unusual interest. 
In one community thirty-five farmers took work once a week for nineteen 
weeks and the result was that all kinds of livestock were standardized and only 
purebreds are being grown now. 
It is the policy of this State to undertake one thing at a time and do it 
well. Our present undeveloped field is part-time work with boys sixteen to 
twenty-one years of age who are out of school. We expect to concentrate on 
this for the year 1923-24. 
Our present need is more money. The demand is growing rapidly for more 
than one man in a county. The possibility of having teachers of Vocational 
Agricultural teachers to train rural school teachers for junior project work is 
very great. People are beginning to see the need of tying the rural schools to 
the Vocational Agricultural Department of the high school. This phase of the 
work will increase from year to year. 
The enrollment in Vocational Agriculture shows 2,807 in the all-day 
schools, 1,264 in the evening work with farmers and 1,195 in the junior project 
work in the rural schools. This makes a total of 5,266 people taking organized 
instruction in Vocational Agriculture. In addition to these the teachers of 
Vocational Agriculture came in personal contact with 121,654 people outside 
of the school room. . 
The project profits in Vocational Agriculture for the year ending June 30, 
1923 were $365,069.02. There was spent for this work during the past year 
$151,108.60. 
The project profits returned $6.21 for every dollar of Federal money spent; 
$9.77 for every dollar of.State money spent; and $6.65 for every dollar of local 
money spent. / 
The white boys earned $199,256.89 in their all-day work. The negroes 
earned $37,892.20. The farmers who attended the evening classes earned 
$111,413.06. The boys attending the rural elementary schools earned 
~ $16,506.87 on their junior projects.

HOME ECONOMICS EDUCATION 
Waite 
While there has not been a large increase in the number of schools carrying 
on Vocational Home Economics there has been a decided advance in the 
quality of the work. It is interesting to find that several principals have 
stated that even though financial aid may not be available for the coming year, 
they are desirous of their schools fulfilling to the letter the requirements set 
up by the Vocational Home Economics Department. . 
The work in Evening Schools has increased nearly 100 per cent. 
A definite program and course of study has been outlined for all day schools 
for the coming year. 
There is a large waiting field for nutrition and health work throughout the 
entire State. 
The program for the coming year has a few changes from that of the past, 
such as requiring two years of related Science of three single and two double 
periods per week. 
: Schools Teachers Pupils 
State Aided___-..--.--.---- i Al 45 1,795 
15 19 783 Federal Aided. -- -- ---- 
1 2 41 
24 20 895 
15 14 436 
  All the schools have been visited once and some have been visited as many 
as four times in instances where aid was needed to enable the teacher to im- 
prove her methods and plans of work. 
Sectional conferences have been held at which the supervisor and teacher 
trainer gave definite instruction and aid as to the organization and adminis- 
tration and the course of study and improved methods. 
- The major activities of the past year have been: 
To try to show to educational people the value of vocational education and to bring about 
better co-operation with the State Department, the Teacher Training Institution and the Princi- 
pals and Superintendents. . 
To revise the course of study and to bring teachers qualifications to a definite standard. 
Three district conferences were held during the fall semester of two days 
duration. . The purpose of these coriferences was to more clearly put before 
_ the teachers the aim of vocational Home Economics, to give the general out 
line of the course of study and some definite improved methods of presenting 
the subject matter to students. Following the general conference, conferences 
were given the individual teacher by both the supervisor and the teacher 
trainer. . 
A two weeks conference for all vocational Home Economics teachers is 
Planned for the last two weeks of June, to be held at the University of 
‘ennessee. . 
The teacher training curriculum has been changed. Fifteen hours are now 
allowed for electives, a three hour course in organization and administration 
of Vocational Home Economics has been added making a total of 9 hours 
credit for Home Economics Education including Practice Teaching. (Nine 
additional hours of education are required for graduation).
-P-A-G-E-178
—178— 
A ten hour ‘course on “The House” has been added to the home manage- 
ment work, thereby increasing the Home Management credits, including 
residence in the Home Management House to 8 hours. 
Students have studied the plan for part time and evening school work and 
have been given the point of view of the work. 
The supervised teaching is being done in a county high school about two 
miles from the University. The work is supervised by the teacher trainer and 
a vocational home economics program typical for this State is carried on. 
One third of the teacher trainer’s time has been devoted to itinerant 
teachers training work. This work has included (1, assisting the State Super- 
visor in the organization of the State work; 2, assisting with the three district 
conferences held by the State Supervisor; 3, visiting teachers in the State and 
assisting them in the planning of courses, purchase and arrangement of equip- 
ment.and methods of teaching). One-half of the schools have been visited by 
the teacher trainer, Practically all of the teachers have been met (by the 
teacher trainer) and the work discussed with them either through conferences 
or visits. 
Two years of vocational experience is required for completion of the voca- 
tional work. This is checked by the teacher trainer. ; 
Supervised teaching is conducted in a county high school which has the 
standard two years Vocational Home Economics program. 
A minimum of 36 double period lessons is required. 
Each student teaches as many phases of the Home Economics work as 
possible. Lessons in Food, Clothing, and Home Management are taught by 
every student. 
H Supervised Home Management is conducted in Home Economics Practice 
‘ouse. 
A two. weeks summer course is planned to be required for all teachers of 
Vocational Home Economics. A six weeks summer course is required for all 
teachers that have been temporarily qualified to teach the work but have not 
met all of the requirements as yet. 
Next year an experienced teacher will be selected for Young High School 
(the training center) as the regular teacher. She will have charge of the work 
at the practice school and the supervision of student teachers and will work 
under the supervision of the teacher trainer. Through this arrangement the teacher trainer will be left free for a whole semester to make visits in the 
State. The instructor in charge of Home Management and Practice Home will 
devote some of her time to teacher training and help with the itinerant work. 
The types of community service work of the Vocational Home Economics 
teachers the past year are: 
‘Teaching a nutrition class in City Hospital. 
Advisor for Girls Reserve. 
Supervised serving luncheon to Rotary Club. 
Supervised serving luncheon to Lion’s Club. 
Supervised serving luncheon to County Boards. 
Served as Vice-President of Parent Teachers’ Association. 
Aided in community fair at School. 
Students discussed Table Service before Mothers Club. 
Judging in County and Community Fairs. 
Demonstrations to Women’s Clubs. 
Demonstrations at Fair to give the people a conception of what Home Economics means. 
Supervised the planning, Preparing, and serving Alumne Banquet. 
Put on campaign for improving the appearance of school buildings. 
Prepared food for exhibit made by Home Demonstration Agent. 
Students made entries at county fairs.
-P-A-G-E-179
—179— 
A great deal of home work has been done but due to the fact that the 
project program had not been definitely worked out and was not thoroughly 
understood by the teachers, no project work has been done this past year. 
One of the principle things to be taken up at the June Conference will be 
the project program for next year. . 
Necro 
The number of all day schools has not increased but there has been an 
improvement in the quality of the work. The number of evening schools has 
almost doubled. 
There is yet much to be done to improve both the subject matter and the 
methods of Home Economics education in the negro schools. 
The plans for next year require that two and one-half hours daily be de- 
voted to Home Economics, this to include Foods, Clothing, Sanitation, Hy- 
giene, Health, Child Care, Home Laundering, and Home Care. 
Supervised practice teaching is done with the high school students enrolled 
in the A. & I. Normal. 
In teacher training a very thorough course in Home Nursing, Dietetics, 
Food Chemistry, Housewifery, Laundering and Supervision is given. 
The student does at least one month of practice teaching together with supervision in some department all the year. Home Nursing is demonstrated 
through the dormitory sick rooms, and care of children for a length of time. 
The care of a home is demonstrated through the dormitory. Here she learns 
the proper arrangement of furniture, and the care and selection of same. 
The school dining hall as well as the practice dining room serve to teach 
table service. Balanced meals and planning of the same are very important 
factors. 
Getting the best results for the amount of money to be spent is a problem 
for the student to work out. 
‘The types of community service work of the Vocational Home Economics 
teachers the past year are: 
Supervised serving of dinners to County Court and County Boards of Education. 
Supervised preparing of special dishes for sick in community. 
Demonstrations at club meetings. 
Made “Kitchen Calls” in community which was the cause of improving sanitary conditions in the kitchens, in the homes, in the community. 
Supervised serving of lunches at Farmers’ Convention. 
Appeared on programs of county teachers meetings talking on Home Economics Subjects. 
Aided in County Fairs. 
A great deal of home work but no project work has been done this year.
-P-A-G-E-180

EAST TENNESSEE STATE NORMAL 
Johnson City, Tennessee, January 1, 1924. 
Hon. P. L. Harnep, State Commissioner of Education, 
State Department of Education, 
Nashville, Tennessee. 
Dear Sir: 
I am handing you herewith annual report of the East Tennessee State 
Normal School for the year beginning September 1, 1922, and ending August 
31, 1923. This report gives detailed information as to enrollment, classifi- 
cation, diplomas, and certificates issued, and financial statement, including 
both state and local receipts and expenditures, together with general review 
and recommendations, as follows: 
   
    
  ENROLLMENT

T.tal Expenditures, Including Balance 
ADDITIONS AND IMPROVEMENTS 
During the year many substantial and needed additions and improvements 
have been made. These include the following: 
(@) Men’s Dormitory. This isa modern fireproof building in every respect, 
containing forty-nine bed rooms, matron’s apartment, reception room, society 
hall, servants’ quarters, bath rooms, store rooms, etc. 
(2) Library. This contains reading room thirty by one hundred feet, 
stack coom forty by thirty feet, recitation room, society hall, storage rooms, 
rest rooms, etc. , 
(c) Gymnasium. This includes playing court fifty by seventy feet, 
spectators’ galleries to accommodate 1,200, physical directors’ rooms, dressing 
rooms, showers, and lockers, each for men and women. . 
(d) Industrial Arts Building. This is a brick building forty by one hundred 
and twenty feet, and includes wood-working shop, mechanical-drawing shop, 
office, and supply rooms. : 
(e) Pantry. An additional pantry at the cafeteria, sixteen by thrity feet. 
(f) The room in the main building before occupied as manual training 
room has been remodeled and converted into a Physics room, including lec- 
ture room, laboratory, supply room, and dark room. 
(g) The room before occupied as a gymnasium has been remodeled and 
converted into a Chemistry room, including lecture room, laboratory for general 
Chemistry, laboratory for advanced Chemistry, supply room, weighing room, 
and locker. Both the Departments of Physics and Chemistry have been fully 
equipped with modern desks and needed apparatus. 
(h) Truck and Tool House. A building twenty by forty feet was erected 
on the barn lot to be used as a truck and tool house. 
(i) Drinking Fountain. A bronze drinking fountain, costing nearly $700, 
was presented to the school by the senior class of 1923. It has been located 
in front of the main building on a large circular concrete base with grass plot 
surrounding it and encircled by a macadamized drive way. 
(7) New concrete walks have been built from the new roadway to all the 
entrances to the men’s dormitory, and other walks have been built connecting 
with other roadways and walks and leading to the new manual arts building 
and library. 
() A macadamized roadway, sixteen feet wide, with a concrete gutter, 
was built from Lake Street in front of the men’s dormitory and connecting 
with the roadways already in the Normal School grounds. 
The above improvements, together with additional equipment for the 
school, have added very materially to the school plant and to facilities for 
more efficient service.
-P-A-G-E-188
—188— 
NeeEps or THE ScHOOL 
In my annual report submitted in 1922 I called attention to the needs of 
ihe school and submitted recommendations that I wish to repeat-here, as 
follows: 
The needs of the school may be classed under two heads: material and 
social. Some of the material needs of the school, as they appear at the present 
time, are as follows: . . 
(a). An Additional Dormitory for Women. The dormitory for women now 
in use accommodates 115 women. This meets the demand of the institution 
only during the fall quarter. For the remainder of the school year, beginning 
January 1, many young women must find rooms in private homes at great 
additional expense and much inconvenience. While it could not be expected 
that dormitory room will be provided for the very large number of women 
who come during the summer quarter, the dormitories should be ample 
enough to accommodate all who attend during the regular year. An additional 
dormitory to accommodate 150 women is now needed. 
(b) Kitchens and Cafeterias. .Our present kitchens .and cafeterias will 
comfortably take care of 400 boarders. Additional room should be provided, 
and must be provided if the students are to be properly cared for ip this 
important department during the spring and summer quarters. If in the 
dormitory recommended for women a kitchen and cafeteria should be pro- 
vided for all the women in the dormitories, leaving the old kitchen and cafe- 
teria for all other students, I believe the arrangement would be satisfactory 
and economical. 
(c) Laundry. At the present time only limited provisions have been made 
for laundry. A building sufficient for this purpose and properly equipped 
should be provided. This is not only desirable from an economic standpoint, 
but from a hygienic one as well. It seems to me that this is an imperative need 
of the school at the present time. 
(d) Athletics and Grand Stand. The new gymnasium will greatly enlarge 
the facilities of the Normal School for health education, recreation, and play; 
but if this important department is to be enlarged, as it should be, an athletic 
field should be developed, with a suitable grand stand and seating accom- 
modations. . 
(e) Barn, While some improvements and enlargements have been made 
on the school barn, additional room is needed. The barn we now have should 
be enlarged to at least twice its present capacity. 
Socrat Nerps 
(a) Department of Home Study and Correspondence Work. The conference 
of Superintendents at its annual session at the State Normal School on June 21, 22, and 23, adopted the following resolution: 
‘We believe that teachers in service should be encouraged to improve their 
education efficiency, in so far as they may be able to do so without interfering 
with their daily tasks as teachers. We recommend that the State Normal 
School establish a Department of Home Study and Correspondence Work 
and that such teachers as may meet the requirements in such a department 
shall be permitted to work out credits in the Normal School. We believe that 
such a department of correspondence study would encourage hundreds of 
teachers in East Tennessee to pursue helpful professional courses while en- 
gaged in teaching, and would later encourage the resident attendance of such 
teachers at the Normal School.” :
-P-A-G-E-189
—189_ 
The establishment of such a department would enable hundreds, if not 
thousands, of teachers to carry on, in connection with their school work, 
professional courses that would add to their efficiency and contribute to their 
working out Normal School certificates and diplomas. Departments of cor- 
. fespondence work have been established in nearly all universities, colleges, 
and normal schools; and approval by the State Board of Education of the 
recommendations made by the Conference of Superintendents would contri- 
bute very largely to the usefulness of the school. 
(b) A Strengthened Course of Study. At the present time the State 
Normal Schools offer courses of study leading to certificates and diplomas and 
teachers’ license as follows: 
(1) The One-year or Academic Course of Study. On the completion of this 
course, the student receives the certificate of the Normal School, and a first- 
class elementary teacher’s license. 
(2) The Two-year Normal Course of Study. On the completion of this 
course of study, the student receives a Normal School certificate, and is issued 
a first-grade high-school license, good in schools below first class. 
(3) The Three-year Normal Course of Study. .On the completion of this 
course the student receives a diploma of the Normal School, and is granted 
first-class high-school license, good in the department in which the student has 
done major or minor work. 
These three courses are intended to supply teachers for elementary schools, 
both rural and city, high schools below first class, and high schools of the 
first class. 
Additional courses should be provided as follows: 
(1) Rural Elementary Teachers. A special course of study designed for 
those who expect to become efficient teachers in rural schools should be pro- 
vided. This course would be differentiated somewhat from the present one- 
year academic course and should require at least four quarters for graduation 
above the completion of a standard high-school course. 
(2) Fourth-year Normal Course. The Normal School should give a four- 
year course of study leading to the bachelor’s degree. ‘Those who complete 
this course should be authorized, by law, to receive, under the general regula- 
tions, license for a county superintendent and license to engage in vocational 
work in agriculture, home economics, and manual training for such students 
as had majored in these departments. 
The fourth-year normal course would give opportunity for those who desire 
to complete their professional training and receive a bachelor’s degree to do 
so in the school in which they had completed three years of college work. At 
the present time quite a number of the graduates of the Normal School, after 
completing the three-year course of study, attend Peabody College, the Uni- 
versity of Tennessee, or some other college or university for the additional 
year necessary to earn a degree. This opportunity should be offered in the 
State Normal Schools. Such addition would not weaken, in any way, the 
Certificate Course of Study, the Two-year Course, or the Three-year Normal 
Course, but would rather strengthen these departments, which would always 
include the larger number of students. The tendency at the present time is 
to authorize Normal Schools to confer the bachelor’s degree. During the 
past few years many Normal Schools have been converted into teachers’ 
colleges; and, in my judgment, the Normal Schools of Tennessee would be 
made more useful, and the cause of public education would be strengthened,
-P-A-G-E-190
—190— 
by the addition of a fourth year to the present three-year course of study, 
and the conversion of the State Normal Schools into State teachers’ colleges. 
This change should be accompanied by the requirement that the entrance re- 
quirements at the State teachers’ colleges should be graduation from a four- 
year high school, giving at least 15 units of standard high-school work. The 
elimination of the two years of high-school work now given, and the addition 
of one year to the Normal Schools, could be made without the employment 
of any additional teachers in the Normal School faculties. 
Respectfully submitted. : 
Sipney G. Gitpreata, President.
-P-A-G-E-191
  —191— 
MIDDLE TENNESSEE STATE NORMAL 
Murfreesboro, Tennessee, January 5, 1924. 
Hon. P. L. Harnep, Commissioner of Education, 
Nashville, Tennessee. 
Dear Sir: 
Iam herewith submitting to you a report of the work of this institution 
from September 1, 1922, to September 1, 1923. The statistical report which is 
herewith enclosed speaks for itself and attests the success of this institution, and 
the contribution which it is making toward the progress and advancement of 
the schools of Tennessee. A great many of the teachers of Middle Tennessee 
have been students of this institution and quite a few of them hold. certificates 
and diplomas. Practically all of those who have attended in the past are living 
up to their obligation of teaching as many years in the State as they have 
attended the institution. Practically allofthe graduates of the institution have 
chosen the work of teaching as a profession and are making it their life work. 
Our attendance has been unusually large and our facilities have been taxed 
to take care of the student body. Our attendance has been distinguished, 
especially by the number of men;and asbest I can gather from statistics the 
percentage of men in this institution is larger than in any Normal School in 
the United States. 
Our new gymnasium, which is a model in its appointments and equipment, 
is contributing very largely to the success of the institution and is enabling us 
to make great progress in the health education, and the physical development 
of the students. Our athletic field is also being developed and with the addi- 
tion of a grand stand and seating accommodations, it will be perfect in its 
arrangements. We hope during the coming year to have sufficient funds from 
the Student Activity Fees to build a grand stand. 
The boys’ dormitory is proving to be a great success and has already 
demonstrated the wisdom of the State Board of Education in supplying this 
needed improvement. It has increased our attendance of men and is now 
about filled to its capacity. 
While we have every reason for congratulations for the splendid facilities 
which are provided here for teacher training, three more buildings are very 
much needed and should be built at the earliest possible date. 
A model and practice school is necessary to the successful work of the 
institution. A model and practice school is really the heart of a Normal 
School. The six rooms in the east end of the Administration Building used 
for our model and practice school is very much needed for other class-room 
work and the demand is so great as to make a separate building for this 
purpose a necessity. 
A practice cottage for our Home Economics Department is now in great 
demand. This can be made self sustaining and will not entail very much ex- 
pense to the institution. 
A library building is also very much needed. With the erection of these 
three buildings, we will then have a model institution for the care and training 
of at least five hundred students during the regular term and one thousand 
students during the summer term. 
Iam gratified to made the above report and realize it is due to the splendid 
support and co-Operation of the State Department of Education and the State 
Board of Education. . 
Respectfully submitted. P. A. Lyon, President.
-P-A-G-E-192

Permit me to advise in connection with the foregoing that during the year, 1922-23, the State Board of Education constructed three additional buildings at the West Tennessee State Normal School out of the proceeds of the sale of bonds authorized by the State. 
A commodious dining hall, a fireproof dormitory for men, and a power plant building constitute the additions to the school property prior to Sep- tember 1, 1923.
-P-A-G-E-196
—196-— 
In 1923 the Shelby County Court donated fifty thousand dollars to the 
West Tennessee State Normal School with which to construct a Practice 
School on the campus of the institution. In consequence of that generous 
gift on the part of Shelby County, which was approved by the people of the 
county at an election held for the purpose of voting bonds for enlarging school 
facilities of Shelby County, a Practice School building is now approaching 
completion on the campus of the West Tennessee State Normal School. The 
gift of fifty thousand dollars has been largely supplemented by the State 
Board of Education. The appropriation of the State Board combined with the 
gift of the County Court guarantees to the West Tennessee State Normal 
School a beautifully appointed building for practice purposes. 
Necessarily, we regard these new buildings as evidences of progress, and 
we are confident that they will add greatly to the efficiency of this institution 
in its efforts to train teachers for West Tennessee. . 
Numerous other gifts have been made by public-spirited friends of the 
institution and these gifts indicate that the school is attracting favorable at- 
tention from many forward-looking citizens. Some books have been added to 
the Library and some equipment to the Departments of Chemistry, Physics, 
and Manual Arts, but with all of these additions our equipment is still definite- 
ly inadequate. 
It gratifies me to conclude this report with the assurance that the faculty 
and student body are diligent in prosecuting their obligations to the State and 
that a spirit of co-operation obtains throughout all departments. 
Respectfully submitted. 
. A. A. Krncannon, President.

REPORT OF THE TENNESSEE AGRICULTURAL AND 
INDUSTRIAL STATE NORMAL SCHOOL 
GENERAL STATEMENT 
To Hon. P. L. Harnep, Commissioner of Education. 
In 1909 the increasing realization of the need of academic and professional 
training for the Negroes, who were to be the leaders of economic, intellectual 
and religious thought throughout the various communities of the state, 
became crystalized into a Normal School for Negroes. The commendable 
work being done by the school, as shown by the activities of its graduates and 
former students, has more than repaid the Legislature and State Board of 
Education for their liberal expenditures of time, energy and money in its 
establishment and maintenance. 
The school is prominently and beautifully located upon an eminence over- 
looking the Cumberland River. Because of its ideal location and its sanitary 
surrounding the health condition is far above the average. Constant work and 
attention since its establishment have enabled the school to transform its 
campus into a thing of beauty. 
A study of the twelve years of its existence reveals some interesting facts. 
From a few partly completed buildings and a faculty of a dozen earnest 
teachers and a handful of students the school has increased in its physical and 
human resources by leaps and bounds, until now it ranks in the front row of 
first-class Normal Schools, in point of buildings, faculty and students as shown 
by the figures that follow. ‘ as 
Puysicat IMPROVEMENTS 
The building of the additions to our two dormitories has been a great 
asset in the matter of making the life of the students more attractive and home 
like and in causing the students to strive to duplicate these conveniences in 
the communities to which they go. 
Women’s Inpustriat Bur.pine 
The new Home Economics Building has been a manifold benefit. First, 
it has relieved the crowded condition in the Main Building by removing the 
women’s industries. Secondly, the work of the Academic Department has 
been more centralized and correlated by closer indentity of its units in one 
building. Thirdly, there is more privacy for the young women in their theory ~ 
and practice work in the Women’s Building. Fourthly, the building provides a 
very suitable place for our dining hall which seats eight hundred people. On 
the Cafeteria Plan, we can easily accommodate 1,500 to 2,000 persons. This 
system is being used for regular and summer sessions, with remarkable success 
both in handling large groups quickly and in satisfied boarders. Fifthly, the 
building is very conveniently located for the passage of the young women to 
and from their dormitory. Sixthly, space is provided for storage in the base- 
ment, and there are practice rooms in all phases of housekeeping on the third 
oor. 
The large gymnasium makes it possible to handle large groups effectively 
for work in physical education, as well as affording space sufficient for indoor 
games of all kinds. During the winter season it constitutes our chief recreation. 
center. ,
-P-A-G-E-200
—200— 
The greenhouse serves as a plant laboratory for class room work, as well 
as a germination and culture area for early vegetables and winter plants. It 
adds also to the general landscape view because of its conspicious position on 
the front campus. 
Heatine System 
The modification and enlargement of our heating system has done much 
to add to the comfort in our buildings. The installation and upkeep of this 
heating plant serves as a very valuable medium of instruction in plumbing, 
steam fitting, electrical technique and other forms of related shopwork. 
Tue Men’s InpustriEs 
The building housing the men’s industries is conveniently located near the © 
Men’s Dormitory and is easily accessable to the farm and other parts of the 
. campus. The industries include Auto-Mechanics, Blacksmithing, Carpentry, 
Masonry, Painting, Engineering, Printing, Shoemaking, and Tailoring. Owing 
to the related character of the work in the several departments students are 
able to gain a wide knowledge of the operations going on in different types of 
shop industries. The shops are steam heated, electric lighted and electrically 
operated; the closeness of the special lines of work makes correlation easily 
possible. 
: Home Economics 
Under the Smith-Hughes Vocational and Teacher Training system we are | 
teaching ‘all phases of Household Economics including Plain Sewing, Dress- 
making, Millinery, Manual Arts, Handicraft, Laundering, Ladies Tailoring, 
Domestic Science, and related work. Graduates of these departments are 
doing effective service as teachers, supervisors and business women through- 
out the state. 
AGRICULTURE 
This institution is the Smith-Hughes Vocational and Teacher Trainer 
Agriculture center for the state. The courses include four years in High 
School Agriculture, two years of Normal in Teacher Training work and a four- 
year College Course for advanced workers in this field. We supply the larger 
percentage of the Vocational Teachers for the schools of the state. 
Orner Sreciat DEPARTMENTS 
In addition to the Smith-Hughes work just enumerated, the school offers 
complete courses in Business, Kindergarten, Physical Education, Manual Arts, 
and Music. Each of these is a certificate and diploma course arranged for 
those showing special aptitude along these lines. 
Lirerary DEPARTMENTS 
The school gives four years training in Academic, two years in Normal 
and four years in College subjects, with special emphasis on their value as 
aids in teaching. Certificates or diplomas of graduation and for teaching are 
issued from each of these departments. 
‘SumMER ScHooL 
_ The growing interest in improvement during service has increased con- 
siderably our attendance during the six weeks Summer School. From a 250
-P-A-G-E-201
—201— 
teacher attendance in 1912, the enrollment has amounted to 1,200 in 1923. So 
many came from Memphis, Shelby County and Chattanooga that special 
trains were run for the Summer School teachers, a circumstance unheard of in 
the history of summer schools anywhere. Most of these teachers have certifi- 
cates and some attend simply for professional inspiration and improvement. 
Many are pursuing advanced work in the Normal and College Department 
looking forward to higher rewards in the teaching profession. 
Our GRADUATES 
The Alumni list from different departments is significant in showing the 
diversity of pursuits being followed by our graduates. Nearly all the Voca- 
tional, Home Economics, and Agriculture teachers, as well as the supervisors 
in various counties, together with workers under the Smith-Lever Law, are 
graduates of this institution. Nearly every school in the state is represented 
in its faculty by at least one of our students or graduates. Unfortunately the 
demand far exceeds our ability to supply. 
Our NEEDS : 
The most pressing needs are a Teacher’s Home, Library Science Hall, and 
Practice School. The growing importance of the institution, its rapidly in- 
creasing attendance and its state wide service would seem to justify this claim 
upon the resources of the state. The addition of these facilities would relieve 
a present crowded condition and open the doors of service here to a greater 
patronage. ; 
Tue FuTURE : 
We heartily thank the Legislature and the State Board for their generosity 
in providing the facilities for better and more far-reaching results through 
theincreased faculty, more buildings and greater equipment. As our resources 
are increased our opportunities become greater; hence our responsibilities are 
more acute. Whatever is provided for keeping will be guarded with the same 
carefulness that has characterized our administration. 
Our prayer is not for easier tasks, but for greater opportunities for service 
Respectfully submitted, 
W. J. Hate, President.
